        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer238836967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16063223661387893418">As I said in an update, this book has some of the most effortless to read prose I've ever encountered, but also seems very worth re-reading.<br/><br/>This story of a father and son's relationship, linked by mentions of the titular phrase, holds so much emotion: love, loss, pain, regret, hope and hopelessness, moments of overwhelming joy followed by inevitable sadness. I found the earlier part of the book absolutely poetic, the latter less so....but I'm unsure if that was the book (the story) or me and my </span>
  <span id="freeText16063223661387893418" style="display:none">As I said in an update, this book has some of the most effortless to read prose I've ever encountered, but also seems very worth re-reading.<br /><br />This story of a father and son's relationship, linked by mentions of the titular phrase, holds so much emotion: love, loss, pain, regret, hope and hopelessness, moments of overwhelming joy followed by inevitable sadness. I found the earlier part of the book absolutely poetic, the latter less so....but I'm unsure if that was the book (the story) or me and my reading and feelings. <br /><br />There is a deep sadness about the book in spite of the moments of joy. I definitely will re-read this book to feel it again and experience Trond's life again. I too wish we knew a bit more about those middle years of his life but would I feel the same about this book and Trond if all was filled in? Trond seems to have given us the central mysteries of his life.</span>

<div id="review_25777219" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25777219" />
    <a title="Lars Guthrie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/757602-lars-guthrie"><img alt="Lars Guthrie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199816668p2/757602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/25777219?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lars Guthrie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lars Guthrie" href="/user/show/757602-lars-guthrie">Lars Guthrie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer25777219" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17663142524387183873">As chilly as its Norwegian setting, Petterson's novel continues to haunt my thoughts weeks after reading it. Its very title and the many allusions to cowboy culture made me think about what frontier and re-invention means if the edge of the world is vast and dramatically sculpted desert that only ends with limitless ocean, or claustrophobic forest that transitions into Arctic ice. But mostly it made me think about no matter how much we think we know about others and ourselves, it's never complet</span>
  <span id="freeText17663142524387183873" style="display:none">As chilly as its Norwegian setting, Petterson's novel continues to haunt my thoughts weeks after reading it. Its very title and the many allusions to cowboy culture made me think about what frontier and re-invention means if the edge of the world is vast and dramatically sculpted desert that only ends with limitless ocean, or claustrophobic forest that transitions into Arctic ice. But mostly it made me think about no matter how much we think we know about others and ourselves, it's never complete nor definite. As Petterson writes, "People like it when you tell them things, in suitable portions, in a modest, intimate tone, and they think they know you, but they do not, they know about you, for what they are let in on are facts, not feelings, not what your opinion is about anything at all, not how what has happened to you and how all the decisions you have made have turned you into who you are. What they do is fill in with their own feelings and assumptions, and they compose a new life which has precious little to do with yours, and that lets you off the hook. No one can touch you unless you yourself want them to. You only have to be polite and smile and keep paranoid thoughts at bay, because they will talk about you no matter how much you squirm, it is inevitable, and you would do the same thing yourself." My highest recommendation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17663142524387183873" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_216818635" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/216818635" />
    <a title="Donna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526809-donna"><img alt="Donna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479723313p2/1526809.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/216818635?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Donna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Donna" href="/user/show/1526809-donna">Donna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer216818635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11503469453484722704">We have had a death in the family which has meant stopping almost everything to pay my respects to Death and Time. I don't know how long they'll be right in the house like this. Maybe until the Peak Freans run out, or until some illuminating memory shakes out of the vault to make sense of the whole; a snow globe marked 'Souvenir of Life on Earth', the light hidden in those falling fake metallic flakes. I do know that whatever it is, Death and TIme will return to their place on the back burner to</span>
  <span id="freeText11503469453484722704" style="display:none">We have had a death in the family which has meant stopping almost everything to pay my respects to Death and Time. I don't know how long they'll be right in the house like this. Maybe until the Peak Freans run out, or until some illuminating memory shakes out of the vault to make sense of the whole; a snow globe marked 'Souvenir of Life on Earth', the light hidden in those falling fake metallic flakes. I do know that whatever it is, Death and TIme will return to their place on the back burner to be taken for granted again like breath itself. <br />So what do you read when the geese are flying over, the leaves are turning, and Time is cleaning your mirrors? I happened to be reading 'the Norwegian book about the father and son'. And I couldn't have been reading a more perfect book for this time. Perhaps that's the recommendation right there; that this book could stand up to this time. It did not become too frivolous so that I had to set it aside, nor was it too complicated or difficult. It had no trickery and very little ego. <br />The truths in this book are stated as sparely and simply as one of Sibelius' piano works, each piece of time whole in itself and then set down in just the right spot until it makes sense to the adult son and the reader. What the son finds out is that there is more than one order for time. There is the order in which it was lived, the order it is remembered (which is often on shuffle), and the special order that is like a puzzle. Work it, and it gives you the big picture, or at least the forest for the trees. Like the characters, the woods have a strong, resinous presence in this book.<br /><br />"...and the wet boggy moss and the sweet, sharp, all-pervading odour of something greater than ourselves and beyond our comprehension; of the forest, which just went on and on to the north and into Sweden and over to Finland and further on the whole way to Siberia, and you could get lost in this forest and a hundred people go searching for weeks without a chance of finding you, and why should that be so bad, I wondered, to get lost here? But I did not know then how serious that thought was." <br /><br />I know those woods. They go to the tree-line and as spare as this book is, he leaves nothing out. There is some logging in this book, Stihl portraits that never get overwrought or silly. He really leaves nothing out.<br />My high opinion of this book may be due to the time I read it, but I don't think so.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11503469453484722704" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2136726387" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3466378907561955611"><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_qEHQgOe9Eo'>https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_qEHQ...</a><br/><br/>OK, I know Asgeir is from Iceland, and not Norway, but that’s the song that I kept going back to while reading this book: it captures the atmosphere of Trond’s story perfectly.<br/><br/>“Out Stealing Horses” is one of those quiet, introspective books that sits there quietly on the shelf, between novels containing much more bombastic stories. But it doesn’t feel intimidated by them: it knows that the story within its pages is subtler, more complex and more haunting t</span>
  <span id="freeText3466378907561955611" style="display:none"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_qEHQgOe9Eo">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_qEHQ...</a><br /><br />OK, I know Asgeir is from Iceland, and not Norway, but that’s the song that I kept going back to while reading this book: it captures the atmosphere of Trond’s story perfectly.<br /><br />“Out Stealing Horses” is one of those quiet, introspective books that sits there quietly on the shelf, between novels containing much more bombastic stories. But it doesn’t feel intimidated by them: it knows that the story within its pages is subtler, more complex and more haunting that those of its neighbors.<br /><br />Our protagonist is an older man, living by himself in a cabin, far from everything, in Norway. When an unexpected encounter with someone he knew from his childhood happens, it unlocks his memories of a series of events that clearly marked the end of his childhood, and the beginning of a new phase of his life.<br /><br />Maybe that doesn’t sound like much, but the complicated emotions that mark such a transition are perfectly captured by Petterson. Who are our parents when they are not parents? Can one action send our life in a completely different direction than what we expected? Could we ever hope to tell that story to anyone and have them understand?<br /><br />I liked reading this story. It doesn’t reinvent the wheel or anything like that, but it brings to life a very particular time and place beautifully and vividly, with just a light veil of maudlin remembrance laid over it.</span>

<div id="review_2584097643" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2584097643" />
    <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt"><img alt="Rebecca McNutt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602390057p2/36977686.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2584097643?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca McNutt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca McNutt" href="/user/show/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt">Rebecca McNutt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=scandinavian">scandinavian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=horse">horse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=friendship">friendship</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36977686-rebecca-mcnutt?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2584097643" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16095475590328425838">Set in the 1940's, <i>Out Stealing Horses</i> is not only a beautiful work of Scandinavian historical literature, but it's also a very nostalgic and brilliant boyhood coming-of-age tale in the vein of <i>Stand By Me</i>. Trond, an elderly gentleman who's still reeling over the loss of his wife in a car accident, finds that his new neighbour is a man he once knew as a young teenager, causing him to reflect back on a strange and cathartic summer where grief and loss are poignantly explored alongside their endea</span>
  <span id="freeText16095475590328425838" style="display:none">Set in the 1940's, <i>Out Stealing Horses</i> is not only a beautiful work of Scandinavian historical literature, but it's also a very nostalgic and brilliant boyhood coming-of-age tale in the vein of <i>Stand By Me</i>. Trond, an elderly gentleman who's still reeling over the loss of his wife in a car accident, finds that his new neighbour is a man he once knew as a young teenager, causing him to reflect back on a strange and cathartic summer where grief and loss are poignantly explored alongside their endearing adventure.<br /><br />While I'd initially heard complaints about this book that it was too boring, I really didn't find it to be so. If anything, it was a classic and timeless journey exploring the more important moments of life, reflecting back not only on simpler times but also on the value of love and friendship. That said, it's not too gushing or sentimental, either. The characters were very well-written and deep, but it's the excellent portrayal of emotion and adventure that gives <i>Out Stealing Horses</i> its own memorable moments. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16095475590328425838" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1701725464" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1701725464" />
    <a title="Deea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8111258-deea"><img alt="Deea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525606324p2/8111258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1701725464?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deea" href="/user/show/8111258-deea">Deea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Deea by: </span>
      Dolors
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8111258-deea?shelf=best-2017">best-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8111258-deea?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1701725464" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3003008485465462819"><blockquote>
  <b>"You decide for yourself when it will hurt."</b>
</blockquote> The image of an egg falling from the top of a tree and then of a nest destroyed in some seconds. The image of life being shattered in the space of some seconds. These two images got imprinted on my retina while reading this book. They haunted me and they still haunt me even now after having finished the book. I don’t know if it’s Petterson’s unique style of writing that gives these images such strength or putting them in the context that he did. O</span>
  <span id="freeText3003008485465462819" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>"You decide for yourself when it will hurt."</b>
</blockquote> The image of an egg falling from the top of a tree and then of a nest destroyed in some seconds. The image of life being shattered in the space of some seconds. These two images got imprinted on my retina while reading this book. They haunted me and they still haunt me even now after having finished the book. I don’t know if it’s Petterson’s unique style of writing that gives these images such strength or putting them in the context that he did. Or maybe both.<br /><br />The experiences that are part of young Trond’s coming of age, remembered by a 67 year old Trond and filtered through his later experiences highly impressed me. There is so much sadness in this book, but it’s a kind of sadness that cannot be confined within the limits of definitions. It’s maybe the sadness determined by the realization that everything we do represents choices that determine chain reactions, the sadness felt by someone who has experienced betrayal and loss, the longing for solitude after you find that you have lost all the anchors in this world, but in the meantime for human compassion, the longing for resting your tired soul, the feeling of experiencing freedom or rather relief only when surrounded by nature. But <b>“we do decide for ourselves when it will hurt.”</b> This is what Trond had learnt from his father and this guides him even now in his old age. Or he thinks it does. This still helps him have a sense of meaning, even if sometimes meaning has the thin texture of illusion. <blockquote>
  <b>“One of my many horrors is to become the man with the frayed jacket and unfastened flies standing at the Co-op counter with egg on his shirt and more too because the mirror in the hall has given up the ghost. A shipwrecked man without an anchor in the world except in his own liquid thoughts where time has lost its sequence.”</b>
</blockquote>No. Old Trond is not that man. He is never going to become that man. He has established a routine, he cleans after he had his meals, he doesn' t neglect his rooms even though he lives alone now, he has a dog for company, nature to soothe his craving to be alone, he has his past to delve into (<b>“If I just concentrate I can walk into memory's store and find the right shelf with the right film and disappear into it....”</b>), he has his Dickens books to enjoy. He is not hurt as <b>“we do decide for ourselves when it will hurt…”</b>. He is not lost, never lost. And yet…<blockquote>
  <b>“People like it when you tell them things, in suitable portions, in a modest, intimate tone, and they think they know you, but they do not, they know about you, for what they are let in on are facts, not feelings, not what your opinion is about anything at all, not how what has happened to you and how all the decisions you have made have turned you into who you are. What they do is they fill in with their own feelings and opinions and assumptions, and they compose a new life which has precious little to do with yours, and that lets you off the hook. No-one can touch you unless you yourself want them to.”</b>
</blockquote> And yet... Do we really decide for ourselves when we get hurt?</span>
  <a data-text-id="3003008485465462819" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2411070076" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2411070076" />
    <a title="Liz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20625013-liz"><img alt="Liz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1563111756p2/20625013.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2411070076?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz" href="/user/show/20625013-liz">Liz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780312427085" href="/book/show/2204441.Out_Stealing_Horses">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2411070076" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4300529833215761644">Not only are Petterson’ words gracefully spun -- and a translation from Norwegian to English at that -- but the story is told brilliantly. In fact it’s not so much told as it is revealed, in a series of elegantly executed flashbacks. When I was done, several things struck me, fell into place if you will, and I went back to reread earlier parts and confirmed that yes, Petterson did articulate this story as perfectly as I’d first thought. <br/><br/>Trond is a complex character and a man of few words. He is </span>
  <span id="freeText4300529833215761644" style="display:none">Not only are Petterson’ words gracefully spun -- and a translation from Norwegian to English at that -- but the story is told brilliantly. In fact it’s not so much told as it is revealed, in a series of elegantly executed flashbacks. When I was done, several things struck me, fell into place if you will, and I went back to reread earlier parts and confirmed that yes, Petterson did articulate this story as perfectly as I’d first thought. <br /><br />Trond is a complex character and a man of few words. He is not always likeable and there’s an air of mystery about him, but I think that’s what makes this story so engaging. During the flashbacks to his youth, the summer he spent with his father, there’s an overall feeling of melancholy, even during moments of happiness. Almost as if knowing what he knows now, he casts that feeling onto his past. Still, no matter what has happened in his life, he maintains that he has been lucky.<br /><br />It’s not every book that can make you think so deeply about a person’s circumstances and the life they’ve lived that lead to where they are. This is the first Petterson for me and I will definitely be seeking out more of his work. After evaluating my reactions to this one, I’m giving it a well-deserved 5 stars. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4300529833215761644" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_20887953" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20887953" />
    <a title="Gregory Baird" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1067337-gregory-baird"><img alt="Gregory Baird" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515353740p2/1067337.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/20887953?book_show_action=true">Apr 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gregory Baird" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gregory Baird" href="/user/show/1067337-gregory-baird">Gregory Baird</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1067337-gregory-baird?shelf=new-york-times-best-of-the-year">new-york-times-best-of-the-year</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1067337-gregory-baird?shelf=2008-booklist">2008-booklist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1067337-gregory-baird?shelf=fiction-literature">fiction-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1067337-gregory-baird?shelf=european">european</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer20887953" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17452961542445219811"><b> Sumptuous Prose, but Largely Redundant </b><br/><br/>Picking up this novel (translated from its original Norwegian), it is easy to understand why “Out Stealing Horses” has earned such high praise from critics; its author, Per Petterson, is a writer of astonishing talent. There are moments where his astute observations and beautiful descriptions sent chills down my spine. Petterson’s depth of understanding for his main character, Trond, is palpable, and he is carefully rendered in an achingly believable portr</span>
  <span id="freeText17452961542445219811" style="display:none"><b> Sumptuous Prose, but Largely Redundant </b><br /><br />Picking up this novel (translated from its original Norwegian), it is easy to understand why “Out Stealing Horses” has earned such high praise from critics; its author, Per Petterson, is a writer of astonishing talent. There are moments where his astute observations and beautiful descriptions sent chills down my spine. Petterson’s depth of understanding for his main character, Trond, is palpable, and he is carefully rendered in an achingly believable portrait of an aging, grieving man. The novel’s setting gets an equally loving respect from Petterson, whose description of Norway’s trees, rivers, and skies should do wonders for the country’s tourism (“I shut my eyes into a squint and looked across the water flowing past below the window, shining and glittering like a thousand stars, like the Milky Way could sometimes do in the autumn rushing foamingly on and winding through the night in an endless stream”). I would compare Petterson’s writing to the heart aching beauty of Pulitzer Prize winner Marilynne Robinson’s prose (her novel “Housekeeping” is every bit as poetic and haunting as this one). The problem I have with this otherwise stellar book is that I feel like I’ve read it before – many times at that.<br /><br />“Out Stealing Horses” finds Trond Sander living in a self-inflicted isolation as he heads into his twilight years. He has given up his former life for a solitary existence partially out of a life-long yearning to be left alone, but mostly out of grief for the sudden death of his beloved wife three years earlier. But when he realizes that his neighbor is a figure from his past it triggers a host of feelings and memories that Trond has been trying to avoid for a long time, and in flashbacks we are taken back with him to the summer of his fifteenth year – a summer that forever altered the course of his life, where friendly games of stealing horses gave way to tragedy and coming of age. Petterson acquits himself well enough in the unspooling of the narrative, but anyone who has ever read a Booker Prize winning novel will find the premise a little too familiar (“The God of Small Things,” “The Sea,” and “The Gathering,” to name only a few, all have a similar premise with the main character reflecting on their tragic past). But the real shame of it is that “Out Stealing Horses” peters out in the climax, leaving it without the oomph that might have distinguished it from those novels. And what we are left with is a painfully standard story told with stunningly beautiful writing. I wanted to like the novel more than I did because of Petterson’s talent as a writer, but the truth is that I just couldn’t shake the boredom in the end. Which is quite a shame, because Petterson has a lot more to offer.<br /><br />Grade: C+</span>
  <a data-text-id="17452961542445219811" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CoVGQRG7z3pY3iS545QpN4kjtcDp5jTQy/TBG53KtZgwoy+TquUGEv/1kNgUYSPoX3xSCCdrureQioeqUIlHAg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Le<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9371944757240708021\" style=\"display:none\">Najambicioznejša Vojnovićeva knjiga do sedaj!\n\nGoran Vojnović je za svoj prvenec Čefurji raus!, ki je postal prodajna uspešnica in je preveden v osem jezikov, leta 2009 prejel nagrado Prešernovega sklada in nagrado kresnik za najboljši roman leta. Leta 2012 je izdal svoj drugi roman Jugoslavija, moja dežela, za katerega je dobil svojega drugega kresnika.\n\nFiga je roman prepletajočih zgodb o Aleksandru in Jani, Vesni in Safetu, pa Jadranu in Anji. In drugih. A predvsem o Jadranu, ki iz mozaika zgodb, sestavlja veliko zgodbo povezav med njimi, da bi razumel svojo. Ker ne razume, zakaj je Anja odšla iz njunega skupnega življenja, si poskuša razložiti, zakaj je, domnevno s pomočjo sumljive stekleničke zdravil, odšel njegov dedek Aleksandar. Poskuša razumeti odhajanje babice Jane, razkroj njenih spominov, njeno pozabo, njeno življenje v svetu demence. Jadran se trudi razumeti odhod očeta Safeta, njegovo izginotje iz Ljubljane v prvem letu balkanske vojne. In svojo mamo Vesno, njeno nerazložljivo jezo na dedka, njeno zamolčano razočaranje nad očetom. Iz drobcev spominov in vedenja, Jadran sestavi veliko zgodbo preteklosti, da bi razumel svojo sedanjost. Ko pa je zgodba sestavljena, vzroki pojasnjeni, povodi osmišljeni in motivi razkriti, spozna, da nas preteklost ne odvezuje odgovornosti za prihodnost.<\/span>\n  <a She sees a way out on the arm of the new pastor, who needs a tie to the community to cull favor for his plan for the old stave church, with its pagan deity effigies and supernatural bells.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/225944.Per_Petterson">About Per Petterson</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/225944.Per_Petterson"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1292588556p3/225944.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/225944.Per_Petterson">Per Petterson
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
608
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer1398266046055487698">Petterson knew from the age of 18 that he wanted to be a writer, but didn't embark on this career for many years - his debut book, the short story collection <i>Aske i munnen, sand i skoa</i>, (<i>Ashes in the Mouth, Sand in the Shoes</i>) was published 17 years later, when Petterson was 35. Previously he had worked for years in a factory as an unskilled labourer, as his parents had done before him, and had als</span>
  <span id="freeText1398266046055487698" style="display:none">Petterson knew from the age of 18 that he wanted to be a writer, but didn't embark on this career for many years - his debut book, the short story collection <i>Aske i munnen, sand i skoa</i>, (<i>Ashes in the Mouth, Sand in the Shoes</i>) was published 17 years later, when Petterson was 35. Previously he had worked for years in a factory as an unskilled labourer, as his parents had done before him, and had also trained as a librarian, and worked as a bookseller.<br />In 1990, the year following the publication of his first novel, Pettersen's family was struck by tragedy - his mother, father, brother and nephew were killed in a fire onboard a ferry.<br />His third novel <i>Til Sibir</i> (<i>To Siberia</i>) was nominated for The Nordic Council's Literature Prize, and his fourth novel <i>I kjølvannet</i> (<i>In the Wake</i>), which is a young man's story of losing his family in the Scandinavian Star ferry disaster in 1990, won the Brage Prize for 2000.<br />His breakthrough, however, was <i>Ut og stjæle hester</i> (<i>Out Stealing Horses</i>) which was awarded two top literary prizes in Norway - the The Norwegian Critics Prize for Literature and the Booksellers’ Best Book of the Year Award.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://us.macmillan.com/author/perpetterson">http://us.macmillan.com/author/perpet...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="1398266046055487698" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;People like it when you tell them things, in suitable portions, in a modest, intimate tone, and they think they know you, but they do not, they know _about_ you, for what they are let in on are facts, not feelings, not what your opinion is about anything at all, not how what has happened to you and how all the decisions you have made have turned you into who you are. What they do is they fill in with their own feelings and opinions and assumptions, and they compose a new life which has precious little to do with yours, and that lets you off the hook. No-one can touch you unless you yourself want them to.&rdquo;</span>
